<div id="review_2251350929" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2251350929" />
    <a title="TS Chan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30279227-ts-chan"><img alt="TS Chan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543800303p2/30279227.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2251350929?book_show_action=true">Dec 10, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="TS Chan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="TS Chan" href="/user/show/30279227-ts-chan">TS Chan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780465062881" href="/book/show/22928871-endurance">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30279227-ts-chan?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30279227-ts-chan?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30279227-ts-chan?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30279227-ts-chan?shelf=physical-owned">physical-owned</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2251350929" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5330710922371238624"><b>I am in awe and in tears.</b><br/><br/>The ill-fated Imperial Trans-Antarctic Expedition aboard the Endurance has got to be one of the most phenomenal survival stories that will last through the ages. The conditions that beset the crew of the Endurance were brutal and harrowing beyond my imagination. I don't think that even a fiction writer could have conjured up the unbelievably horrific circumstances that were endured, and survived, by Shackleton and his men. <br/><br/>On the book itself, the narrative was written by</span>
  <span id="freeText5330710922371238624" style="display:none"><b>I am in awe and in tears.</b><br /><br />The ill-fated Imperial Trans-Antarctic Expedition aboard the Endurance has got to be one of the most phenomenal survival stories that will last through the ages. The conditions that beset the crew of the Endurance were brutal and harrowing beyond my imagination. I don't think that even a fiction writer could have conjured up the unbelievably horrific circumstances that were endured, and survived, by Shackleton and his men. <br /><br />On the book itself, the narrative was written by piecing together all the events experienced by the crew of the Endurance, accomplished via combing through every diary kept during the expedition and extensive interviews with almost all the surviving crew members. I believe this enabled the author to put forth an in-depth and intimate account of this most incredible and perilous voyage, which lasted almost two years. The audiobook narration added to the experience as the narrator was adept in modulating his delivery to suit the moment - be it desperation or acquiescence, excitement or relief. He also injected a bit of character to the different crew members through nuanced voices. <br /><br />I highly, <i>highly</i> recommend this classic true tale of survival to anyone who seeks to be inspired by the sheer indefatigable will, courage and nobility that humans can be capable of. I wasn't aware that it was even possible until I've read this book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5330710922371238624" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14164562998624080339">This was exciting! I recommend this book to those who want to throw themselves into another world, albeit a world cold, wet, icy and filled with fear, exhaustion and hunger. <br/><br/>Ernest Shackleton set out in 1914 to cross the Antarctic from west to east. Yes, WW1 had broken out and he had Churchill’s go-ahead Why? For the glory of Britain and for his own glory too. The race for polar discovery was in full-swing. On December 14, 1911, the Norwegian explorer Roald Amundsen led the first successful expe</span>
  <span id="freeText14164562998624080339" style="display:none">This was exciting! I recommend this book to those who want to throw themselves into another world, albeit a world cold, wet, icy and filled with fear, exhaustion and hunger. <br /><br />Ernest Shackleton set out in 1914 to cross the Antarctic from west to east. Yes, WW1 had broken out and he had Churchill’s go-ahead Why? For the glory of Britain and for his own glory too. The race for polar discovery was in full-swing. On December 14, 1911, the Norwegian explorer Roald Amundsen led the first successful expedition to arrive at the South Pole, five weeks ahead of a British party led by Robert Falcon Scott. Robert Edwin Peary, an American explorer, is credited with having been the first to reach the geographic North Pole. There has been some debate as to whether Frederick Cook, also an American, got there a year earlier.<br /><br />The audiobook narration by Simon Prebble is excellent.<br /><br />When the expedition began there were twenty-nine men aboard the Endurance; there was one stowaway! <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[All twenty-nine survived.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>This book lets you live the experiences of these men and shows how this amazing feat was accomplished. I have a shelf for books concerning “bad-trip” expeditions. To date, this is my favorite.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_273120383" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/273120383" />
    <a title="Brandon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3214163-brandon"><img alt="Brandon" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603283104p2/3214163.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/273120383?book_show_action=true">Feb 06, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brandon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brandon" href="/user/show/3214163-brandon">Brandon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Brandon by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/1413439-stephen">Stephen</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3214163-brandon?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3214163-brandon?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3214163-brandon?shelf=biographies">biographies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3214163-brandon?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3214163-brandon?shelf=memoir">memoir</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer273120383" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8549405779345386938">Ernest Shackleton and his crew of 27 men have taken away my man card. Seriously.<br/><br/>I have never even remotely come into a situation as dire and desperate as the stranded crew of the Endurance. If I did, I’m sure I would curl up in the fetal position and just hope that it all works itself out. That being said, it wouldn’t be long before I would freeze to death or be killed and eaten by a sea leopard. However, if Shackleton was my captain, he could probably get more out of me then I would think possi</span>
  <span id="freeText8549405779345386938" style="display:none">Ernest Shackleton and his crew of 27 men have taken away my man card. Seriously.<br /><br />I have never even remotely come into a situation as dire and desperate as the stranded crew of the Endurance. If I did, I’m sure I would curl up in the fetal position and just hope that it all works itself out. That being said, it wouldn’t be long before I would freeze to death or be killed and eaten by a sea leopard. However, if Shackleton was my captain, he could probably get more out of me then I would think possible.<br /><br />In 1915, Shackleton and his crew were attempting to become the first to navigate the continent. Little did they know, they would run into some hellish circumstances that would push them to their absolute limit. Shortly after embarking on their arduous journey, the ship becomes trapped. Stuck for months, the ship eventually becomes crushed under the pressure of the surrounding ice. Faced with a "do or die" situation, Shackleton makes the call to lead the men in search of dry land.<br /><br />For the life of me, I still cannot understand how they survived. However, I do realize that until you’re faced with a situation like this, you’re unable to imagine how you’ll react. Between the loss of limbs, the dire food situations, the difficult living conditions and the fear of never knowing if you'll make it or not, I don't know how they did it.<br /><br />All of these men have an iron will, there's simply no other way to explain it. It made me think about my day to day problems and how little they matter when you contrast it to being stranded at sea and living on ice flows for the better part of two years.<br /><br />As good as this book was, I did feel it dragged at parts. I grabbed the audiobook of this and while Simon Prebble's narration was excellent, I found parts were a little long winded. This is in no way a criticism of a real life situation, just maybe a tighter narrative would have been better.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8549405779345386938" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1594279415" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12645828594218753099">This is a straightforward, perhaps even understated, telling of Ernest Shackleton's failed 1914 Trans-Antarctic Expedition that led to a lengthy (over a year) battle against the elements for survival, including a crossing of the Drake passage in a 22-foot whaleboat using only rudimentary navigation equipment and a never-before-attempted overland crossing of treacherous South Georgia island with only a small length of rope and a carpenter's adze. Writing in the 1950s, Lansing was able to suppleme</span>
  <span id="freeText12645828594218753099" style="display:none">This is a straightforward, perhaps even understated, telling of Ernest Shackleton's failed 1914 Trans-Antarctic Expedition that led to a lengthy (over a year) battle against the elements for survival, including a crossing of the Drake passage in a 22-foot whaleboat using only rudimentary navigation equipment and a never-before-attempted overland crossing of treacherous South Georgia island with only a small length of rope and a carpenter's adze. Writing in the 1950s, Lansing was able to supplement his review of journal accounts by conducting first-hand interviews with many of the expedition's survivors. Yet at times the account feels somewhat under-detailed, with little to no account given to the short glorious life of Mrs. Chippy, the threat of Elephant Island survivors considering cannibalism, the lack of Polar Medals given to several members of the crew after the journey due to their lack of effort and/or insubordination, and various other noteworthy details.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12645828594218753099" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9334518173328285363"><i>For scientific leadership give me Scott; for swift and efficient travel, Amundsen; but when you are in a hopeless situation, when there seems no way out, get down on your knees and pray for Shackleton.</i><br/><br/>A story not only of survival, but of the boundless will to live. I was amazed at the tenacity not to succumb to what presented itself as an impossibly bleak circumstance: marooned on ice in the Weddell Sea during the approaching Arctic Night. My favorite character by far was the Drake Passage, whic</span>
  <span id="freeText9334518173328285363" style="display:none"><i>For scientific leadership give me Scott; for swift and efficient travel, Amundsen; but when you are in a hopeless situation, when there seems no way out, get down on your knees and pray for Shackleton.</i><br /><br />A story not only of survival, but of the boundless will to live. I was amazed at the tenacity not to succumb to what presented itself as an impossibly bleak circumstance: marooned on ice in the Weddell Sea during the approaching Arctic Night. My favorite character by far was the Drake Passage, which turned out to be just a bastard of an antagonist. My wife has agreed to read this next and I cannot wait to plan when our first meal of “hoosh” will be. Very simply put, <i>Endurance</i> is Alfred Lansing’s ode to the men who lived through an ordeal most of us get anxious only reading about. As for me, I’ll never complain about being cold again, for I’ve never really been cold—<i>Endurance</i> taught me the meaning of that word.</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1931242155" />
    <a title="Andy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2199404-andy"><img alt="Andy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1331458678p2/2199404.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1931242155?book_show_action=true">Mar 03, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andy" href="/user/show/2199404-andy">Andy</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1931242155" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13015760513684012796">I'm sorry. I know this is an incredible survival story, but I have to call bullshit. This is being advertised as a story of "leadership" and "heroism" when it's more like the opposite. I used to be OK with letting such stuff go as a "conversation starter" but I now think such fuzziness is too dangerous. <br/><br/>Shackleton plans the expedition for the sake of a publicity stunt that he hopes will make him rich. He picks his crew "capriciously." He "hypocritically" adds a science component. He ignores advi</span>
  <span id="freeText13015760513684012796" style="display:none">I'm sorry. I know this is an incredible survival story, but I have to call bullshit. This is being advertised as a story of "leadership" and "heroism" when it's more like the opposite. I used to be OK with letting such stuff go as a "conversation starter" but I now think such fuzziness is too dangerous. <br><br>Shackleton plans the expedition for the sake of a publicity stunt that he hopes will make him rich. He picks his crew "capriciously." He "hypocritically" adds a science component. He ignores advice from whalers. He fails to bring along essential materials. He lands his ship in a self-created disaster. And so on. I give the author credit for presenting these facts and labels, but the overall tone is off. <br><br>This book is assigned in schools. We should not be teaching young people that leadership is selfishness, hubris, poor planning, bad decision-making, etc. Saving your own skin in a difficult situation could be admirable resourcefulness, but it's not the best illustration of heroism. <br><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/588138.The_Hero_With_a_Thousand_Faces" title="The Hero With a Thousand Faces by Joseph Campbell" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/books/1442885694s/588138.jpg" alt="The Hero With a Thousand Faces by Joseph Campbell" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="13015760513684012796" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2519446701" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8884138925633414994"><b> <i>Endurance: Shackleton's Incredible Voyage</i> is a heartfelt story that is deeply touching, and rewards the reader with a true story of survival against all odds, and the strength of the human spirit!</b><br/><br/><br/><u>Alfred Lansing's book about the incredible misadventure of the explorer ship, The Endurance, is nothing short of a masterpiece!</u> This thrilling account of one of the most astonishing feats of exploration and human courage ever seen, and to top it off, its' 100% based on real events. The author, Alfred L</span>
  <span id="freeText8884138925633414994" style="display:none"><b> <i>Endurance: Shackleton's Incredible Voyage</i> is a heartfelt story that is deeply touching, and rewards the reader with a true story of survival against all odds, and the strength of the human spirit!</b><br><br><br><u>Alfred Lansing's book about the incredible misadventure of the explorer ship, The Endurance, is nothing short of a masterpiece!</u> This thrilling account of one of the most astonishing feats of exploration and human courage ever seen, and to top it off, its' 100% based on real events. The author, Alfred Lansing, wrote this 61 years ago, and it feels so incredibly relevant to modern day standards. Lansing himself, was a WW2 veteran and served in the capacity of a Naval hero, receiving the purple heart for his efforts! His military background likely inspired him to write his magnum opus. Having experienced true life and death moments, likely gave him the substrate to relate to each character of this extraordinary voyage of the Endurance. <br><br> In a nutshell, the story tells the adventures of the British ship Endurance, which set sail In August of 1914 for the South Atlantic. Along with his seasoned ship captain <i>Ernest Shackleton</i>, a group of 30 odd sailors embarked in the adventure of a lifetime, in the hopes of conquering malignant seas, and being the first recorded ship to sail to Antarctica. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1546836806i/26876982._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><br><b> A heart wrenching tale of shipwreck in the least hospitable place on earth, makes the Endurance an absolute must read! </b>Lansing's prose is majestic, he's narrative is affluent in describing the perils of the ill-fated crew. Lansing's 'cadence' and tonality makes his book so effective and powerful. Along the book, real photos archived form the Royal Geography society, made the experienced that much more real! <br><br>As a reader, I couldn't put down the book, as the turn of a single page could have been doom or salvation for our brave sailors! Without spoilers (this is in the book's description), the 'Endurance' becomes trapped in 2 feet floes of ice in the middle of the Antartic sea. What follows is a series of death-defying attempts of escape and survival! When I thought the worst was over...holy shit, I so wrong! Also, having no previous knowledge as to the story's conclusion really helped heightened my experience! <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1546836806i/26876983._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><br> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1546837110i/26876995._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="320" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><br><br><b> <i>Endurance: Shackleton's Incredible Voyage</i> is an awe inspiring tale of brave sailors, that faced the unimaginable, and with death throes all around them, they taught us the unbreakable strenght of the human spirit!<br><br>5 STARS!</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="8884138925633414994" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer5261101" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8293308954747812567">I rarely re-read books, but this was such a gripping, true-life adventure, and well told, that I have read it three times in the last fifteen years. I think it is due for another re-read! It blows my mind to think of what these men went through and how they survived being stuck in Antarctica for years. The way they engineer their own rescue is hardly to be believed. What adds to the appeal of the book is the wonderful color throughout - details of how the men made life bearable during this unbea</span>
  <span id="freeText8293308954747812567" style="display:none">I rarely re-read books, but this was such a gripping, true-life adventure, and well told, that I have read it three times in the last fifteen years. I think it is due for another re-read! It blows my mind to think of what these men went through and how they survived being stuck in Antarctica for years. The way they engineer their own rescue is hardly to be believed. What adds to the appeal of the book is the wonderful color throughout - details of how the men made life bearable during this unbearably stressful and gruelling experience - playing football on the ice, putting on Christmas Eve talent shows, passing the time by making a list of the foods they'd most like to eat when they are stranded on a desolate rock with nothing to eat but seal blubber. With a few choice details and diary excerpts, Lansing also makes almost all of the men come alive on the page as very distinct (and often quirky) characters. Awesome book!</span>
  <a data-text-id="8293308954747812567" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer7691089635301223889">A major book retailer used to list this book as Number One among "Winter Sports." That was a mistake, because this amazing, grueling and true account of men against the elements is anything but *sportif*. ENDURANCE recounts, instead, the sufferings of a crew of British sailors who set out in 1914 to be the first to traverse the Antarctic continent, but whose ship became marooned in ice, forcing them to find an alternate route back to civilization over difficult ice and dangerous water, bit by ag</span>
  <span id="freeText7691089635301223889" style="display:none">A major book retailer used to list this book as Number One among "Winter Sports." That was a mistake, because this amazing, grueling and true account of men against the elements is anything but *sportif*. ENDURANCE recounts, instead, the sufferings of a crew of British sailors who set out in 1914 to be the first to traverse the Antarctic continent, but whose ship became marooned in ice, forcing them to find an alternate route back to civilization over difficult ice and dangerous water, bit by agonizing bit. <br /><br />Based on meticulous research and actual diary entries, Alfred Lansing's remarkable ENDURANCE is a landmark in the nonfiction adventure genre. I can recommend it to anyone. The customary version has a foreword by Dr. James Dobson. <br /><br />[review updated June 13, 2020] </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3266500408" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3266500408" />
    <a title="Quo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7392004-quo"><img alt="Quo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1393910036p2/7392004.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3266500408?book_show_action=true">Apr 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Quo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Quo" href="/user/show/7392004-quo">Quo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Unknown Binding 9781885283009" href="/book/show/3819495-endurance">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7392004-quo?shelf=interpersonal-dynamics">interpersonal-dynamics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7392004-quo?shelf=nature-environment">nature-environment</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7392004-quo?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7392004-quo?shelf=wilderness-adventure">wilderness-adventure</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3266500408" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9946885355167329980">Alfred Lansing's <i>Endurance: Shackleton's Incredible Voyage</i> is the story of a great quest on the part of some very adventuresome men, commanded by a man who acted in a manner that was very much against the grain of British leadership for his time & place and showed amazing strength of character & resilience. <br/><br/>Beyond that however, the book represents a labor of love on the part of the author, someone who engendered a literary quest to master as many of the details as possible about Ernest Shackleto</span>
  <span id="freeText9946885355167329980" style="display:none">Alfred Lansing's <i>Endurance: Shackleton's Incredible Voyage</i> is the story of a great quest on the part of some very adventuresome men, commanded by a man who acted in a manner that was very much against the grain of British leadership for his time &amp; place and showed amazing strength of character &amp; resilience. <br><br>Beyond that however, the book represents a labor of love on the part of the author, someone who engendered a literary quest to master as many of the details as possible about Ernest Shackleton &amp; those still alive who were part of that great adventure to the Antarctic at the outset of WWI. This most perilous journey began just as the the voyage seemed very much in doubt due to the onset of war, with the First Lord of the Admiralty, Winston Churchill sending Shackleton a one word response: "Proceed!" <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1589758353i/29496006.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The men on board were a diverse lot that included, well-trained officers Frank Worsley (captain) &amp; Frank Wild (2nd-in-command), 3 other officers, an experienced navigator, 2 surgeons, a geologist, meteorologist, biologist, physicist, an official artist, a mix of "able-seamen", a carpenter, a motor expert (former P.E. coach), 2 fireman, the cook &amp; even a stowaway, later pressed into service to assist the ship's cook. <br><br>Frank Hurley, whose photographs were said to have been "the soul of the expedition", was described as difficult to deal with but others included a devout Presbyterian who read the bible daily &amp; the physicist, an intellectual named Reginald James, who seemed out-of-place &amp; kept to himself. Mostly, the motley group that sheltered together for so very long within the cramped ship &amp; then in tents on unstable ice floes, got along well enough together, many establishing a lifelong bond. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1589855583i/29500860.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>As anyone familiar with the expedition knows by now, the <i>Endurance</i>, a 3 masted vessel called a <i>barkentine</i>, failed to reach its intended destination, for while the ship was<blockquote>a beautiful vessel by any standards &amp; "the strongest wooden ship ever built in Norway", it eventually became encased in pack ice, drifting 130+ miles in a matter of weeks, as "one microscopic speck 144 feet long &amp; 25 feet wide" embedded in nearly one million square miles of ice that was being slowly rotated by the irresistible clockwise sweep of the winds &amp; currents of the Weddell Sea.</blockquote>Kenneth Johnson, a marine geologist I met on board the <i>Marco Polo</i> on my voyage to Antarctica commented that what seemed to doom the <i>Endurance</i> was a kind of convergence of strong winds above &amp; exceedingly strong currents below the ice, causing dynamic shifts in the ice &amp; subjecting the ship to a kind of pincers effect. Ultimately, the <i>Endurance</i> is crushed &amp; as one man said, "she had carried us so far &amp; so well and then put up the bravest fight that any ship had fought before yielding to the remorseless pack". The ship had been a symbol that linked them to the outside world &amp; to civilization, complete with a gramophone, now putting the men at the mercy of the ever-drifting ice. <br><br>As food supplies began to diminish, the men were forced to endure not just the Antarctic blizzards &amp; a winter with no light but a diet of seal meat, augmented with stewed penguin heart, liver, tongues &amp; "God knows what else, washed down with a cup of water". At one point a giant walrus chasing one of the men on the ice was shot &amp; it became a valuable source of nourishment as well. <br><br>Much of the book details the voyage of 3 small boats salvaged from their crushed ship to South Georgia, after a long &amp; perilous journey amidst dismal weather conditions &amp; dangerous ice floes, with Shackleton leading these carefully chosen men to land &amp; gradually to the safety of an encampment on South Georgia following an additional overland trek. Eventually Enrest Shackleton returned to rescue the men he'd left behind, succeeding in gaining them transport back to England without the loss of a single soul. <br><br>On board the vessel I sailed on to Antarctica was a wonderful man named Chris Wilson, whose great-uncle, Edward Wilson had sailed with Shackleton &amp; who died with Robert Falcon Scott on a later expedition to Antarctica. Chris described the era of the voyage of the <i>Endurance</i> as "a time when men were made of iron &amp; ships of wood, with the opposite being the case today." <br><br>Shackleton's voyage is sometimes dismissively called "audacious", which seems the appropriate word to the author who then comments, "if not, it wouldn't have been to Shackleton's liking, for he was an explorer in the classic mold--utterly self-reliant, romantic &amp; just a little swashbuckling." Indeed, "Shackleton was no ordinary individual &amp; what might have seemed an act of reasonable caution to the average person was to Shackleton a detestable admission that failure was a possibility." One of the men who served with Shackleton put it this way:<blockquote>For scientific leadership, give me Scott; for safe &amp; efficient travel, Amundsen; but when you are in a hopeless situation, when there seems no way out, get down on your knees &amp; pray for Shackleton.</blockquote> <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1589758353i/29496007.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>In my reading of Lansing's book, the plight of the <i>Endurance</i> is a tale of just that, endurance, but also a story of Shackleton's leadership in the face of almost unspeakable hardship &amp; uncertainty...<blockquote>Many of them, it seemed, finally grasped for the first time how utterly powerless they were. Shackleton strove so unceasingly to imbue them with a basic faith in themselves, that they could, if need be, pit their strength &amp; determination against any obstacle--and somehow overcome it.</blockquote> <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1589829616i/29499657._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>After doing some research into the background of the author, I discovered that Alfred Lansing had once worked for a suburban newspaper with offices in my village, walking distance from my home, this after serving in the U.S. Navy for 6 years and then gaining a journalism degree from Northwestern University. <br><br>After eventually moving east, he became so invested with the remarkable story of the <i>Endurance</i>, that he left his family behind on Long Island while using his own funds to scour Great Britain in an effort to record the stories of the remaining participants in the voyage Shackleton had instigated &amp; the subsequent struggle for survival, with Lansing's own quest resulting in a most memorable book, one I recommend highly.<br><br>*The book contains many black &amp; white atmospheric photos of the expedition Shackleton led to Antarctica, taken by Frank Hurley. **The newspaper ad within my review is most likely fictitious. And as a sort of bookmark tucked into my copy of <i>Endurance</i> is an official First-Day-Cover of colorful stamps, entitled "Shackleton's Dogs", with a sketch of the <i>Endurance</i> in the background, obtained via the British Antarctic Territory.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9946885355167329980" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1146750?book_show_action=true">May 10, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Davie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Davie" href="/user/show/43599-davie">Davie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43599-davie?shelf=finished">finished</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1146750" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2294078439370186304">Part ripping yarn, part social psychology experiment, part metaphor for graduate school -- this is the most IN-CREDIBLE book that I've ever read.<br/><br/>It is insaaaaaannnneeeeee!!!! <br/><br/>But even though the whole time you are reading it, you are thinking to yourself -- #$(*&@$%(*waaahhh!!! -- the writing is remarkably restrained and pithy, putting you right in the moment without distracting you from what's going on. You'd almost think you were reading Updike, except the people in the story are real, and the</span>
  <span id="freeText2294078439370186304" style="display:none">Part ripping yarn, part social psychology experiment, part metaphor for graduate school -- this is the most IN-CREDIBLE book that I've ever read.<br /><br />It is insaaaaaannnneeeeee!!!! <br /><br />But even though the whole time you are reading it, you are thinking to yourself -- #$(*&amp;@$%(*waaahhh!!! -- the writing is remarkably restrained and pithy, putting you right in the moment without distracting you from what's going on. You'd almost think you were reading Updike, except the people in the story are real, and they are really trying to sail 1,000 miles in open sea in a 20 foot open-topped boat, and deciding whether or not it's worth it to slide down a fog-shrouded mountaintop to be smashed against rocks or fly off the edge of a cliff. <br /><br />Alfred Lansing tells it straight, with lots of quotes from the sailors' diaries, and brilliant structuring. I hope he got some kind of prize for this, since it kicks so much ass. <br /><br />!!!More exclamation points!!!<br />!!!!!</span>
  <a data-text-id="2294078439370186304" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('OwKnVa6hi9LgPFtQnWNs8WOg5OkqHmA4R4Nu4sVpxXyJeAcc619q/+vbXawVY5LNMrHrCvmZ4x1huQSrBwDnBw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('wB9/NAZiQqNb6RCr/mU0A1yLEfUNyEsBAyfKF8yXLytyZd99Q5yjjlAOFld2Zco/DZoeFt5PyCQlHaBeDv4NUA==')})
  }

With a tremendous cracking and splintering of oak, it struck the ship just beneath the anchor secured at the cat-head on the port bow...&quot; \n\nIn the Heart of the Sea brings to new life the incredible story of the wreck of the whaleship Essex - an event as mythic in its own century as the Titanic disaster in ours, and the inspiration for the climax of Moby-Dick. In a harrowing page-turner, Nathaniel Philbrick restores this epic story to its rightful place in American history.\n\nIn 1820, the 240-ton Essex set sail from Nantucket on a routine voyage for whales. Fifteen months later, in the farthest reaches of the South Pacific, it was repeatedly rammed and sunk by an eighty-ton bull sperm whale. Its twenty-man crew, fearing cannibals on the islands to the west, made for the 3,000-mile-distant coast of South America in three tiny boats. During ninety days at sea under horrendous conditions, the survivors clung to life as one by one, they succumbed to hunger, thirst, disease, and fear.\n\nPhilbrick interweaves his account of this extraordinary ordeal of ordinary men with a wealth of whale lore and with a brilliantly detailed portrait of the lost, unique community of Nantucket whalers. Impeccably researched and beautifully told, the book delivers the ultimate portrait of man against nature, drawing on a remarkable range of archival and modern sources, including a long-lost account by the ship\'s cabin boy.\n\nAt once a literary companion and a page-turner that speaks to the same issues of class, race, and man\'s relationship to nature that permeate the works of Melville, In the Heart of the Sea will endure as a vital work of American history.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"1252067307473187422\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n Weaving a treacherous path through the freezing Weddell Sea, they had come within eighty-five miles of their destination when their ship, Endurance, was trapped fast in the ice pack. Soon the ship was crushed like matchwood, leaving the crew stranded on the floes. Their ordeal would last for twenty months, and they would make two near-fatal attempts to escape by open boat before their final rescue.Drawing upon previously unavailable sources, Caroline Alexander gives us a riveting account of Shackleton\'s expedition--one of history\'s greatest epics of survival. And she presents the astonishing work of Frank Hurley, the Australian photographer whose visual record of the adventure has never before been published comprehensively. Together, text and image re-create the terrible beauty of Antarctica, the awful destruction of the ship, and the crew\'s heroic daily struggle to stay alive, a miracle achieved largely through Shackleton\'s inspiring leadership. The survival of Hurley\'s remarkable images is scarcely less miraculous: The original glass plate negatives, from which most of the book\'s illustrations are superbly reproduced, were stored in hermetically sealed cannisters that survived months on the ice floes, a week in an open boat on the polar seas, and several more months buried in the snows of a rocky outcrop called Elephant Island. Finally Hurley was forced to abandon his professional equipment; he captured some of the most unforgettable images of the struggle with a pocket camera and three rolls of Kodak film.Published in conjunction with the American Museum of Natural History\'s landmark exhibition on Shackleton\'s journey, The Endurance thrillingly recounts one of the last great adventures in the Heroic Age of exploration--perhaps the greatest of them all.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"286193161941537448\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div They could never have imagined what awaited them. \n\nTheir nightmare began with a dreadful shipwreck off the coast of Africa, a hair-raising confrontation with hostile native tribesmen within hours of being washed ashore, and a hellish confinement in a rickety longboat as they tried, without success, to escape the fearsome coast. Eventually captured by desert nomads and sold into slavery, Riley and his men were dragged along on an insane journey through the bone-dry heart of the Sahara—a region unknown to Westerners. Along the way the Americans would encounter everything that could possibly test them: barbarism, murder, starvation, plagues of locusts, death, sandstorms that lasted for days, dehydration, and hostile tribes that roamed the desert on armies of camels. They would discover ancient cities and secret oases. They would also discover a surprising bond between a Muslim trader and an American sea captain, men who began as strangers, were forced to become allies in order to survive, and, in the tempering heat of the desert, became friends—even as the captain hatched a daring betrayal in order to save his men. \n\nFrom the cold waters of the Atlantic to the searing Saharan sands, Skeletons on the Zahara is a spectacular odyssey through the extremes.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/29034.Alfred_Lansing">About Alfred Lansing</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/29034.Alfred_Lansing"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_111x148-e08b4682eea7088f8cdfd67c131d51cd.png);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/29034.Alfred_Lansing">Alfred Lansing
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
99
followers
</div>

</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer13330578630868511260">An American journalist who wrote for Collier's, among other magazines and was later an editor for Time, Inc. Books.<br/><br/>Alfred Lansing served in the US Navy from 1940-46. He received the Purple Heart for his wartime service. <br/><br/>Later he attended North Park College, 1946-48, Northwestern University, 1948-50. <br/><br/>Lansing became a member of the Scott Polar Research Institute, Cambridge, England in 1957.<br/><br/><br/></span>

</div>
